<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Chinese philosopher, founder of Taoism</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the book also known as Laozi, see <a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Laozi</b> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">老子</span>, <small>Mandarin: </small><span class="IPA" lang="cmn-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[làu.tsɹ̩]</a></span>; commonly translated as "Old Master"), also rendered as <b>Lao Tzu</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lao-tzu_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lao-tzu-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lao_Tzu_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lao_Tzu-3">[3]</a></sup> or <b>Lao-Tze</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> proper name <b>Li Er</b>, courtesy name <b>Boyang</b>, was an ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosopher</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_writers" title="Chinese writers">writer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubei_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubei-5">[5]</a></sup> He is the reputed author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-6">[6]</a></sup> the founder of philosophical <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_gods" title="Chinese gods">deity</a> in religious Taoism and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_religion" title="Traditional Chinese religion">traditional Chinese religions</a>.
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_legend" title="Chinese legend">semi-legendary</a> figure, Laozi is usually portrayed as a 6th-century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span> contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a>. Some modern historians consider him to have lived during the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> of the 4th century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn4-7">[7]</a></sup> A central figure in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a>, Laozi is claimed by both the emperors of the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> and modern people of the <a href="/wiki/Li_(surname_%E6%9D%8E)" title="Li (surname 李)">Li surname</a> as a founder of their lineage. Laozi's work has been embraced by various <a href="/wiki/Anti-authoritarianism" title="Anti-authoritarianism">anti-authoritarian</a> movements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and has had a profound impact on subsequent Chinese philosophers, who have both commended and criticized his work extensively.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Laozi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Laozi itself is a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_honorifics" title="Chinese honorifics">Chinese honorific title</a>: <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%80%81" title="wikt:老">老</a></span></span> (<small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old</a></small> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i>r<a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">ˤu</a> <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">ʔ</a></i>, "old, venerable")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxsag_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-9">[9]</a></sup> and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%90" title="wikt:子">子</a></span></span> (<small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old</a></small> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i>tsə<a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">ʔ</a></i>, "master").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxsag_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-9">[9]</a></sup> In traditional accounts, <i>Laozi'</i>s actual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_personal_names" title="Chinese personal names">personal name</a> is given as Er <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%80%B3" title="wikt:耳">耳</a></span></span>,</span> <small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old</a></small> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i> nə<a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">ʔ</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxsag_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-9">[9]</a></sup> <small><a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Mod.</a></small><i> Ěr</i>) and his <a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">courtesy name</a> as <a href="/wiki/Bogar" title="Bogar">Boyang</a> <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">trad.</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BC%AF" title="wikt:伯">伯</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%99%BD" title="wikt:陽">陽</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">simp.</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BC%AF" title="wikt:伯">伯</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%98%B3" title="wikt:阳">阳</a></span></span>,</span> <small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old</a></small> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i>P<a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">ˤrak</a>-lang</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxsag_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-9">[9]</a></sup> <small><a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Mod.</a></small> <i>Bóyáng</i>), with his <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">surname</a> being <a href="/wiki/Li_(surname_%E6%9D%8E)" title="Li (surname 李)">Li</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%8E" title="wikt:李">李</a></span></span>; <i>Lǐ</i>). A prominent <a href="/wiki/Posthumous_name" title="Posthumous name">posthumous name</a> was <i>Dan</i> <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%81%83" title="wikt:聃">聃</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Dān</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> in his biography mentions his name as <i>Lǎo Dān</i> <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%80%81" title="wikt:老">老</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%81%83" title="wikt:聃">聃</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Lǎo Dān</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LDR_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-LDR-13">[13]</a></sup> The name <i>Lǎodān</i> also appears interchangeably with <i>Lǎozi</i> in early Daoist texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LDR_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-LDR-13">[13]</a></sup> and may also be the name by which <i>Laozi</i> was addressed by <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> when they possibly met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LDR_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-LDR-13">[13]</a></sup> According to the <i>Companion Encyclopedia of Asian Philosophy</i>,  "the 'founder' of philosophical Daoism is the quasi-legendary <i>Laodan</i>, more commonly known as <i>Laozi</i> (Old Master)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The honorific title <i>Laozi</i> has been <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Chinese" title="Romanization of Chinese">romanized</a> numerous ways, sometimes leading to confusion. Increasingly common in the 21st century is <i>Laozi</i>, which is based on the <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngoog_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ngoog-15">[15]</a></sup> From around the 1920s to the 1990s, <i>Lao Tzu</i> was the most common romanization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngoog_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-ngoog-15">[15]</a></sup> In the 19th century, the title was usually romanized as <i>Lao-tse</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngoog_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-ngoog-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Other forms include the hyphenated variants <i>Lao-tze</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <i>Lao-tsu.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_views">Historical views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Laozi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the mid-twentieth century, a consensus emerged among scholars that the <a href="/wiki/Historicity" title="Historicity">historicity</a> of the person known as Laozi is doubtful and that the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> was "a compilation of Taoist sayings by many hands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The earliest certain reference to the present figure of Laozi is found in the 1st‑century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> collected by the historian <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> from earlier accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In one account, Laozi was said to be a contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> during the 6th or 5th century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>. His personal name was Er or Dan. He was an official in the imperial archives and wrote a book in two parts before departing to the west. In another, Laozi was a different contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> titled Lao Laizi <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%80%81" title="wikt:老">老</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%8E%B1" title="wikt:莱">莱</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%90" title="wikt:子">子</a></span></span>)</span> and wrote a book in 15 parts. In a third, he was the court astrologer Lao Dan who lived during the 4th century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span> reign of the <a href="/wiki/Duke_Xian_of_Qin_(424%E2%80%93362_BC)" title="Duke Xian of Qin (424–362 BC)">Duke Xian of Qin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The oldest text of the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> so far recovered was part of the <a href="/wiki/Guodian_Chu_Slips" title="Guodian Chu Slips">Guodian Chu Slips</a>. It was written on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bamboo_slips" title="Bamboo slips">bamboo slips</a>, and dates to the late 4th century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to traditional accounts, Laozi was a scholar who worked as the Keeper of the Archives for the royal court of <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> This reportedly allowed him broad access to the works of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Yellow Emperor</a> and other classics of the time. The stories assert that Laozi never opened a formal school but nonetheless attracted a large number of students and loyal disciples. There are many variations of a story retelling his encounter with Confucius, most famously in the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpkins_1999_pp_12-13_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simpkins_1999_pp_12-13-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was born in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Village_(China)" title="Village (China)">village</a> before the Qin and Han dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> He was said to be the son of the Censor-in-Chief of the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> and Lady Yishou (益壽氏). In accounts where Laozi married, he was said to have had a son who became a celebrated soldier of <a href="/wiki/Wei_(state)" title="Wei (state)">Wei</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>.
</p><p>The story tells of Zong the Warrior who defeats an enemy and triumphs, and then abandons the corpses of the enemy soldiers to be eaten by vultures. By coincidence Laozi, traveling and teaching the way of the Tao, comes on the scene and is revealed to be the father of Zong, from whom he was separated in childhood. Laozi tells his son that it is better to treat respectfully a beaten enemy, and that the disrespect to their dead would cause his foes to seek revenge. Convinced, Zong orders his soldiers to bury the enemy dead. Funeral mourning is held for the dead of both parties and a lasting peace is made.
</p><p>Many clans of the Li family trace their descent to Laozi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woolf2007_1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007_1-28">[28]</a></sup> including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_China" title="List of rulers of China">emperors</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woolf2007_1_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007_1-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hargett2006_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hargett2006-30">[30]</a></sup> This family was known as the Longxi Li lineage (<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%9A%B4%E8%A5%BF%E6%9D%8E%E6%B0%8F" title="zh:隴西李氏">隴西李氏</a>). According to the Simpkinses, while many (if not all) of these lineages are questionable, they provide a testament to Laozi's impact on Chinese culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The third story in <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> states that Laozi grew weary of the moral decay of life in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chengzhou" title="Chengzhou">Chengzhou</a> and noted the kingdom's decline. He ventured west to live as a hermit in the unsettled frontier at the age of 80. At the western gate of the city (or kingdom), he was recognized by the guard <a href="/wiki/Yinxi" title="Yinxi">Yinxi</a>. The sentry asked the old master to record his wisdom for the good of the country before he would be permitted to pass. The text Laozi wrote was said to be the <i>Tao Te Ching</i>, although the present version of the text includes additions from later periods. In some versions of the tale, the sentry was so touched by the work that he became a disciple and left with Laozi, never to be seen again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In others, the "Old Master" journeyed all the way to India and was the teacher of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siddartha_Gautama" title="Siddartha Gautama">Siddartha Gautama</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>. Others say he was the Buddha himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpkins_1999_pp_12-13_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simpkins_1999_pp_12-13-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>A seventh-century work, the <i>Sandong Zhunang</i> ("Pearly Bag of the Three Caverns"), embellished the relationship between Laozi and Yinxi. Laozi pretended to be a farmer when reaching the western gate, but was recognized by <a href="/wiki/Yinxi" title="Yinxi">Yinxi</a>, who asked to be taught by the great master. Laozi was not satisfied by simply being noticed by the guard and demanded an explanation. Yinxi expressed his deep desire to find the <i>Tao</i> and explained that his long study of astrology allowed him to recognize Laozi's approach. Yinxi was accepted by Laozi as a disciple. This is considered an exemplary interaction between Taoist master and disciple, reflecting the testing a seeker must undergo before being accepted. A would-be adherent is expected to prove his determination and talent, clearly expressing his wishes and showing that he had made progress on his own towards realizing the <i>Tao</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Pearly Bag of the Three Caverns</i> continues the parallel of an adherent's quest. Yinxi received his ordination when Laozi transmitted the <i>Tao Te Ching</i>, along with other texts and precepts, just as Taoist adherents receive a number of methods, teachings and scriptures at ordination. This is only an initial ordination and Yinxi still needed an additional period to perfect his virtue, thus Laozi gave him three years to perfect his Tao. Yinxi gave himself over to a full-time devotional life. After the appointed time, Yinxi again demonstrates determination and perfect trust, sending out a black sheep to market as the agreed sign. He eventually meets again with Laozi, who announces that Yinxi's immortal name is listed in the heavens and calls down a heavenly procession to clothe Yinxi in the garb of immortals. The story continues that Laozi bestowed a number of titles upon Yinxi and took him on a journey throughout the universe, even into the nine heavens. After this fantastic journey, the two sages set out to western lands of the barbarians. The training period, reuniting and travels represent the attainment of the highest religious rank in medieval Taoism called "Preceptor of the Three Caverns". In this legend, Laozi is the perfect Taoist master and Yinxi is the ideal Taoist student. Laozi is presented as the <i>Tao</i> personified, giving his teaching to humanity for their salvation. Yinxi follows the formal sequence of preparation, testing, training and attainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The story of Laozi has taken on strong religious overtones since the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>. As <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> took root, Laozi was worshipped as a god. Belief in the revelation of the <i>Tao</i> from the divine Laozi resulted in the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Celestial_Masters" title="Way of the Celestial Masters">Way of the Celestial Masters</a>, the first organized religious Taoist sect. In later mature Taoist tradition, Laozi came to be seen as a personification of the <i>Tao</i>. He is said to have undergone numerous "transformations" and taken on various guises in various incarnations throughout history to initiate the faithful in the Way. Religious Taoism often holds that the "Old Master" did not disappear after writing the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> but rather spent his life traveling and revealing the <i>Tao</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn_3-4_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn_3-4-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">Taoist myths</a> state that Laozi was conceived when his mother gazed upon a falling star. He supposedly remained in her womb for 62 years before being born while his mother was leaning against a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plum_tree" title="Plum tree">plum tree</a>. (The Chinese surname <a href="/wiki/Li_(surname_%E6%9D%8E)" title="Li (surname 李)">Li</a> shares its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">character</a> with "plum".) Laozi was said to have emerged as a grown man with a full grey beard and long earlobes, both symbols of wisdom and long life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Other myths state that he was <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reborn</a> 13 times after his first life during the days of <a href="/wiki/Fuxi" title="Fuxi">Fuxi</a>. In his last incarnation as Laozi, he lived nine hundred and ninety years and spent his life traveling to reveal the <i>Tao</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn_3-4_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn_3-4-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 156px"><div style="width: 156px">

</p>

</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tao_Te_Ching">Tao Te Ching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Laozi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Tao Te Ching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">Wu wei</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_(16135526115).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="2132" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_%2816135526115%29.jpg/170px-Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_%2816135526115%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_%2816135526115%29.jpg/255px-Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_%2816135526115%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_%2816135526115%29.jpg/340px-Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_%2816135526115%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ping_Sien_Si_-_016_Lao_zi_(16135526115).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Laozi Immortal and Grand Master of Heaven</div></div></div>
<p>Laozi is traditionally regarded as the author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a></i> (<i>Daodejing</i>), though the identity of its author(s) or compiler(s) has been debated throughout history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> It is one of the most significant treatises in Chinese <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">cosmogony</a>. As with most other ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophers</a>, Laozi often explains his ideas by way of paradox, analogy, appropriation of ancient sayings, repetition, symmetry, rhyme, and rhythm. In fact, the whole book can be read as an analogy – the ruler is the awareness, or self, in meditation and the myriad creatures or empire is the experience of the body, senses and desires.
</p><p>The <i>Tao Te Ching</i>, often called simply <i>Laozi</i> after its reputed author, describes the <i>Dao</i> (or <i>Tao</i>) as the source and ideal of all existence: it is unseen, but not transcendent, immensely powerful yet supremely humble, being the root of all things. People have desires and free will (and thus are able to alter their own nature). Many act "unnaturally", upsetting the natural balance of the Tao. The <i>Tao Te Ching</i> intends to lead students to a "return" to their natural state, in harmony with Tao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Language and <a href="/wiki/Conventional_wisdom" title="Conventional wisdom">conventional wisdom</a> are critically assessed. Taoism views them as inherently biased and artificial, widely using paradoxes to sharpen the point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn-22_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn-22-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Livia Kohn provides an example of how Laozi encouraged a change in approach, or return to "nature", rather than action. Technology may bring about a false sense of progress. The answer provided by Laozi is not the rejection of technology, but instead seeking the calm state of <i>wu wei</i>, free from desires. This relates to many statements by Laozi encouraging rulers to keep their people in "<a href="/wiki/Ignorance" title="Ignorance">ignorance</a>", or "simple-minded". Some scholars insist this explanation ignores the religious context, and others question it as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apologetic" title="Apologetic">apologetic</a> of the philosophical coherence of the text. It would not be unusual political advice if Laozi literally intended to tell rulers to keep their people ignorant. However, some terms in the text, such as "valley spirit" (<i>gushen</i>) and "soul" (<i>po</i>), bear a metaphysical context and cannot be easily reconciled with a purely ethical reading of the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn-22_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn-22-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">Wu wei</a></i> (無為), literally "non-action" or "not acting", is a central concept of the <i>Tao Te Ching</i>. The concept of <i>wu wei</i> is multifaceted, and reflected in the words' multiple meanings, even in English translation; it can mean "not doing anything", "not forcing", "not acting" in the theatrical sense, "creating nothingness", "acting spontaneously", and "flowing with the moment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is a concept used to explain <i><a href="/wiki/Ziran" title="Ziran">ziran</a></i> (自然), or harmony with the <i>Tao</i>. It includes the concepts that value distinctions are ideological and seeing ambition of all sorts as originating from the same source. Laozi used the term broadly with <a href="/wiki/Simplicity" title="Simplicity">simplicity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humility" title="Humility">humility</a> as key virtues, often in contrast to selfish action. On a political level, it means avoiding such circumstances as war, harsh laws and heavy taxes. Some Taoists see a connection between <i>wu wei</i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esoteric" title="Esoteric">esoteric</a> practices, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Zuowang" title="Zuowang">zuowang</a></i> "sitting in oblivion" (emptying the mind of bodily awareness and thought) found in the Zhuangzi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn-22_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn-22-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taoism">Taoism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Laozi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Taoism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a></div>
<p>Laozi is traditionally regarded as the founder of Taoism, intimately connected with the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> and "primordial" (or "original") Taoism. Popular ("religious") Taoism typically presents the <a href="/wiki/Jade_Emperor" title="Jade Emperor">Jade Emperor</a> as the official head <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>. Intellectual ("elite") Taoists, such as the <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Celestial_Masters" title="Way of the Celestial Masters">Celestial Masters sect</a>, usually present Laozi (<i>Laojun</i>, "Lord Lao") and the <a href="/wiki/Three_Pure_Ones" title="Three Pure Ones">Three Pure Ones</a> at the top of the pantheon of deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a religious figure, he is worshipped under the name "Supreme Old Lord" or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taishang_Laojun" title="Taishang Laojun">Taishang Laojun</a> <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">太上老君</span></span></span>, <i>Tàishàng Lǎojūn</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and as one of the "<a href="/wiki/Three_Pure_Ones" title="Three Pure Ones">Three Pure Ones</a>".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Laozi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi (book)</a></div>
<p>Potential officials throughout Chinese history drew on the authority of non-Confucian sages, especially Laozi and <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_Zhou" title="Zhuang Zhou">Zhuangzi</a>, to deny serving any ruler at any time. Zhuangzi, Laozi's most famous follower in traditional accounts, had a great deal of influence on Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrats" title="Scholar-bureaucrats">literati</a> and culture. Lao Tsu influenced millions of Chinese people by his psychological understanding. He persuaded people by his inaction and non-speaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Political theorists influenced by Laozi have advocated humility in leadership and a restrained approach to statecraft, either for ethical and pacifist reasons, or for tactical ends. In a different context, various anti-authoritarian movements have embraced the Laozi teachings on the power of the weak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Laozi was a proponent of limited government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Left-libertarianism" title="Left-libertarianism">Left-libertarians</a> in particular have been influenced by Laozi – in his 1937 book <i><a href="/wiki/Nationalism_and_Culture" title="Nationalism and Culture">Nationalism and Culture</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalism" title="Anarcho-syndicalism">anarcho-syndicalist</a> writer and activist <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Rocker" title="Rudolf Rocker">Rudolf Rocker</a> praised Laozi's "gentle wisdom" and understanding of the opposition between political power and the cultural activities of the people and community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In his 1910 article for the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a> also noted that Laozi was among the earliest proponents of essentially <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchist</a> concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> More recently, anarchists such as John P. Clark and <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a> have written about the conjunction between anarchism and Taoism in various ways, highlighting the teachings of Laozi in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In her rendition of the Tao Te Ching, Le Guin writes that Laozi "does not see political power as magic. He sees rightful power as earned and wrongful power as usurped... He sees sacrifice of self or others as a corruption of power, and power as available to anyone who follows the Way. No wonder anarchists and Taoists make good friends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Right-libertarianism" title="Right-libertarianism">right-libertarian</a> economist <a href="/wiki/Murray_Rothbard" title="Murray Rothbard">Murray Rothbard</a> suggested that Laozi was the first <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> likening Laozi's ideas on government to <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_order" title="Spontaneous order">spontaneous order</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> James A. Dorn agreed, writing that Laozi, like many 18th-century liberals, "argued that minimizing the role of government and letting individuals develop spontaneously would best achieve social and economic harmony."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Cato_Institute" title="Cato Institute">Cato Institute</a>'s <a href="/wiki/David_Boaz" title="David Boaz">David Boaz</a> includes passages from the <i>Tao Te Ching'</i> in his 1997 book <i>The Libertarian Reader</i> and noted in an article for the <i>Encyclopædia Britannica</i> that Laozi advocated for rulers to "do nothing" because "without law or compulsion, men would dwell in harmony."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boaz_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boaz-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Philosopher Roderick Long argues that libertarian themes in Taoist thought are actually borrowed from earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian" title="Confucian">Confucian</a> writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles_conferred_by_Tang_dynasty">Titles conferred by Tang dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Laozi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Titles conferred by Tang dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> in 666, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong</a> honored him as "Supremely Mysterious and Primordial Emperor" <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">太上玄元皇帝</span></span>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 743, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong</a> honored him the <a href="/wiki/Temple_name" title="Temple name">temple name</a> of a Sage <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">聖祖</span></span>)</span> and granted him the <a href="/wiki/Posthumous_name" title="Posthumous name">posthumous title</a> "Mysterious and Primordial Emperor" <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">玄元皇帝</span></span>)</span>. Emperor Xuanzong also granted his parents the posthumous titles of Congenital Emperor <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">先天太上皇</span></span>)</span> and Congenital Empress <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">先天太后</span></span>)</span> respectively.
</p><p>In 749, he was honored as "Sage of Great Tao and Mysterious and Primordial Emperor" <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">聖祖大道玄元皇帝</span></span>)</span>. In 754, he was given a highest honor of the "Great Sage of Highest Great Tao of the Golden Order and Mysterious and Primordial Heavenly Great Emperor" <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">大聖祖高上大道金闕玄元天皇大帝</span></span>)</span>.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 239.33333333333px"><div style="width: 239.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 237.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confucius_and_Laozi,_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County,_Shandong_province,_China.jpg" title="A Western Han (202 BCE – 9 CE) fresco depicting Confucius and Laozi, from a tomb of Dongping County, Shandong, China"><img alt="A Western Han (202 BCE – 9 CE) fresco depicting Confucius and Laozi, from a tomb of Dongping County, Shandong, China" data-file-height="304" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Confucius_and_Laozi%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg/356px-Confucius_and_Laozi%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e4/Confucius_and_Laozi%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg 1.5x" width="238"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215.33333333333px"><div style="width: 215.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 213.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laozi_002.jpg" title="A stone sculpture of Laozi, located north of Quanzhou at the foot of Mount Qingyuan"><img alt="A stone sculpture of Laozi, located north of Quanzhou at the foot of Mount Qingyuan" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Laozi_002.jpg/320px-Laozi_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Laozi_002.jpg/480px-Laozi_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Laozi_002.jpg 2x" width="214"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

<li id="cite_note-stanford-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-stanford_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-stanford_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/laozi/" rel="nofollow">"Laozi"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>. Stanford University. 2018. <q>The discovery of two <i>Laozi</i> silk manuscripts at Mawangdui, near Changsha, Hunan province in 1973 marks an important milestone in modern <i>Laozi</i> research. The manuscripts, identified simply as 'A' (jia) and 'B' (yi), were found in a tomb that was sealed in 168 BC. The texts themselves can be dated earlier, the 'A' manuscript being the older of the two, copied in all likelihood before 195 BC.<br/>"Until recently, the Mawangdui manuscripts have held the pride of place as the oldest extant manuscripts of the <i>Laozi</i>. In late 1993, the excavation of a tomb (identified as M1) in Guodian, Jingmen city, <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>, has yielded among other things some 800 bamboo slips, of which 730 are inscribed, containing over 13,000 Chinese characters. Some of these, amounting to about 2,000 characters, match the <i>Laozi</i>. The tomb...is dated around 300 BC.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Laozi&amp;rft.btitle=Stanford+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy&amp;rft.pub=Stanford+University&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fplato.stanford.edu%2Fentries%2Flaozi%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALaozi"></span></span>

<li><a href="/wiki/Essence-Function" title="Essence-Function">Yòng</a>: Function</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhengming" title="Zhengming">Zhèng míng</a>: Rectification of names</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zhi" title="Zhi">Zhì</a>: Intention or will; Wisdom or cleverness</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ziran" title="Ziran">Zìrán</a>: Self-so or natural</li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="background:#cfce99;;width:1%">Topics</th><td class="navbox-list-with-group navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0"><div style="padding:0 0.25em">
